nan	we have a highly heterogeneous host inventory -- multiple vendors, multiple levels of CPUs. I have had very little trouble installing and updating  our entire range of hosts. In fact, the hypervisor installer is one or the best installers out there -- fast, prompts are nearly impossible to misinterpret and incompatibility warnings are presented early enough to halt an upgrade without damaging the existing version. On the down side, when there is a driver that setup doesn't like it doesn't offer enough detail to figure out which driver is causing the stop or what driver/version setup was expecting. The vCenter server installer for Windows  installer, however, is far less friendly, to the point that if  a new vCenter installation fails to start the only choices are a long easter egg hunt for the reason or a full re-install of the OS. I know that the Windws version of vCenter has been discontinued, but I expect that the team that designed the installer still works for VMware and the design philosophies that made the Windows vCenter setup so much tweakier than the ESXi setup are still being applied on other products.	nan	nan	nan	nan	nan	nan	nan	nan

nan	The last 12 months have been a nightmare of substandard releases due to insufficient or poor QA. I have been a VMware administrator of small and large environments since the early days of GSX and this is without a doubt the most frustrating period I have been through in those 20 years. I have spent an inordinate amount of time patching, opening GSS cases, unpatching, rolling back, and reinstalling vSphere on new boot devices due to the sloppy, poorly documented (initially) rollout of VMFS-L in ESXi 7.x with respect to SD and USB-based media devices. USB-based boot devices have been a supported method for DECADES on this platform only to suddenly dumped rather unceremoniously with NO forewarning - and yes I am aware that guidance now states "will be unsupported in a future major release", but that was NOT the guidance after issues were first reported. Our 100% virtualized large infrastructure environment is completely diskless and this retrofit-requiring change will have massive financial and opportunity cost implications for our organization. vSphere has always been a rock-solid platform for enterprise virtualization requiring minimal day-to-day care and feeding - which has allowed me some focus on initiatives that advance my business' other goals, but this past year I have spent the entirety of my time dealing with the vSphere issues mentioned above. Want to know why we're not experimenting with Tanzu yet? With VMW on AWS yest? With NSX yet? Because ALL of my current time is devoted to simply keeping our core vSphere 7.x environment stable as a result of issues that should never have made it past QA! I'm hopeful these issues will pass soon given the tacit admission by VMware (by recalling them) that these releases were not up to normal VMware standards, but this past year has been a major black eye for vSphere as an enterprise infrastructure product in my opinion.	nan	nan	nan	nan	nan	nan	nan	nan
